19 In front of the house he observed a bird that had been caught in some bushes , and this he put into his wallet beside the cheese .

1 He fetched some bread out of a cupboard , cut a round off the loaf , and spread the jam on it .
2 `` That wo n't taste amiss , '' he said ; `` but I 'll finish that waistcoat first before I take a bite . ''
3 He placed the bread beside him , went on sewing , and out of the lightness of his heart kept on making his stitches bigger and bigger .
4 In the meantime the smell of the sweet jam rose to the ceiling , where heaps of flies were sitting , and attracted them to such an extent that they swarmed on to it in masses .
5 `` Ha !
6 who invited you ? ''
7 said the tailor , and chased the unwelcome guests away .
8 But the flies , who did n't understand English , refused to let themselves be warned off , and returned again in even greater numbers .
9 At last the little tailor , losing all patience , reached out of his chimney corner for a duster , and exclaiming : `` Wait , and I 'll give it to you , '' he beat them mercilessly with it .
10 When he left off he counted the slain , and no fewer than seven lay dead before him with outstretched legs .
11 `` What a desperate fellow I am ! ''
12 said he , and was filled with admiration at his own courage .
13 `` The whole town must know about this '' ; and in great haste the little tailor cut out a girdle , hemmed it , and embroidered on it in big letters , `` Seven at a blow . ''
14 `` What did I say , the town ?
15 no , the whole world shall hear of it , '' he said ; and his heart beat for joy as a lamb wags his tail .
16 The tailor strapped the girdle round his waist and set out into the wide world , for he considered his workroom too small a field for his prowess .
17 Before he set forth he looked round about him , to see if there was anything in the house he could take with him on his journey ; but he found nothing except an old cheese , which he took possession of .
18 In front of the house he observed a bird that had been caught in some bushes , and this he put into his wallet beside the cheese .
19 Then he went on his way merrily , and being light and agile he never felt tired .
20 His way led up a hill , on the top of which sat a powerful giant , who was calmly surveying the landscape .
21 The little XXXXX went up to him , and greeting him cheerfully said : `` Good-day , friend ; there you sit at your ease viewing the whole wide world .	tailor		anything|blow|fellow|girdle|jam|landscape|letters|smell|tailor|world

9 The little tailor went up to him , and greeting him cheerfully said : `` Good-day , friend ; there you sit at your ease viewing the whole wide world .
10 I 'm just on my way there .
11 What do you say to accompanying me ? ''
12 The giant looked contemptuously at the tailor , and said : `` What a poor wretched little creature you are ! ''
13 `` That 's a good joke , '' answered the little tailor , and unbuttoning his coat he showed the giant the girdle .
14 `` There now , you can read what sort of a fellow I am . ''
15 The giant read : `` Seven at a blow '' ; and thinking they were human beings the tailor had slain , he conceived a certain respect for the little man .
16 But first he thought he 'd test him , so taking up a stone in his hand , he squeezed it till some drops of water ran out .
17 `` Now you do the same , '' said the giant , `` if you really wish to be thought strong . ''
18 `` Is that all ? ''
19 said the little tailor ; `` that 's child 's play to me , '' so he dived into his wallet , brought out the cheese , and pressed it till the whey ran out .
20 `` My squeeze was in sooth better than yours , '' said he .
21 The XXXXX did n't know what to say , for he could n't have believed it of the little fellow .	giant		beings|child|fellow|giant|nothing|play|read|town|wallet|world

2 `` That 's a good joke , '' answered the little tailor , and unbuttoning his coat he showed the giant the girdle .
3 `` There now , you can read what sort of a fellow I am . ''
4 The giant read : `` Seven at a blow '' ; and thinking they were human beings the tailor had slain , he conceived a certain respect for the little man .
5 But first he thought he 'd test him , so taking up a stone in his hand , he squeezed it till some drops of water ran out .
6 `` Now you do the same , '' said the giant , `` if you really wish to be thought strong . ''
7 `` Is that all ? ''
8 said the little tailor ; `` that 's child 's play to me , '' so he dived into his wallet , brought out the cheese , and pressed it till the whey ran out .
9 `` My squeeze was in sooth better than yours , '' said he .
10 The giant did n't know what to say , for he could n't have believed it of the little fellow .
11 To prove him again , the giant lifted a stone and threw it so high that the eye could hardly follow it .
12 `` Now , my little pigmy , let me see you do that . ''
13 `` Well thrown , '' said the tailor ; `` but , after all , your stone fell to the ground ; I 'll throw one that wo n't come down at all . ''
14 He dived into his wallet again , and grasping the bird in his hand , he threw it up into the air .
15 The bird , enchanted to be free , soared up into the sky , and flew away never to return .
16 `` Well , what do you think of that little piece of business , friend ? ''
17 asked the tailor .
18 `` You can certainly throw , '' said the giant ; `` but now let 's see if you can carry a proper weight . ''
19 With these words he led the tailor to a huge oak tree which had been felled to the ground , and said : `` If you are strong enough , help me to carry the tree out of the wood . ''
20 `` Most certainly , '' said the little tailor : `` just you take the trunk on your shoulder ; I 'll bear the top and branches , which is certainly the heaviest part . ''
21 The giant laid the trunk on his XXXXX , but the tailor sat at his ease among the branches ; and the giant , who could n't see what was going on behind him , had to carry the whole tree , and the little tailor into the bargain .	shoulder		coat|creature|hand|man|shoulder|sky|sort|squeeze|stone|wallet

1 `` Is that all ? ''
2 said the little tailor ; `` that 's child 's play to me , '' so he dived into his wallet , brought out the cheese , and pressed it till the whey ran out .
3 `` My squeeze was in sooth better than yours , '' said he .
4 The giant did n't know what to say , for he could n't have believed it of the little fellow .
5 To prove him again , the giant lifted a stone and threw it so high that the eye could hardly follow it .
6 `` Now , my little pigmy , let me see you do that . ''
7 `` Well thrown , '' said the tailor ; `` but , after all , your stone fell to the ground ; I 'll throw one that wo n't come down at all . ''
8 He dived into his wallet again , and grasping the bird in his hand , he threw it up into the air .
9 The bird , enchanted to be free , soared up into the sky , and flew away never to return .
10 `` Well , what do you think of that little piece of business , friend ? ''
11 asked the tailor .
12 `` You can certainly throw , '' said the giant ; `` but now let 's see if you can carry a proper weight . ''
13 With these words he led the tailor to a huge oak tree which had been felled to the ground , and said : `` If you are strong enough , help me to carry the tree out of the wood . ''
14 `` Most certainly , '' said the little tailor : `` just you take the trunk on your shoulder ; I 'll bear the top and branches , which is certainly the heaviest part . ''
15 The giant laid the trunk on his shoulder , but the tailor sat at his ease among the branches ; and the giant , who could n't see what was going on behind him , had to carry the whole tree , and the little tailor into the bargain .
16 There he sat behind in the best of spirits , lustily whistling a tune , as if carrying the tree were mere sport .
17 The giant , after dragging the heavy weight for some time , could get on no further , and shouted out : `` Hi !
18 I must let the tree fall . ''
19 The tailor sprang nimbly down , seized the tree with both hands as if he had carried it the whole way and said to the giant : `` Fancy a big lout like you not being able to carry a tree ! ''
20 They continued to go on their way together , and as they passed by a cherry tree the giant grasped the top of it , where the ripest fruit hung , gave the branches into the tailor 's hand , and bade him eat .
21 But the little XXXXX was far too weak to hold the tree down , and when the giant let go the tree swung back into the air , bearing the little tailor with it .	tailor		air|branches|cheese|eye|ground|part|tailor|time|trunk|wallet

1 `` Well thrown , '' said the tailor ; `` but , after all , your stone fell to the ground ; I 'll throw one that wo n't come down at all . ''
2 He dived into his wallet again , and grasping the bird in his hand , he threw it up into the air .
3 The bird , enchanted to be free , soared up into the sky , and flew away never to return .
4 `` Well , what do you think of that little piece of business , friend ? ''
5 asked the tailor .
6 `` You can certainly throw , '' said the giant ; `` but now let 's see if you can carry a proper weight . ''
7 With these words he led the tailor to a huge oak tree which had been felled to the ground , and said : `` If you are strong enough , help me to carry the tree out of the wood . ''
8 `` Most certainly , '' said the little tailor : `` just you take the trunk on your shoulder ; I 'll bear the top and branches , which is certainly the heaviest part . ''
9 The giant laid the trunk on his shoulder , but the tailor sat at his ease among the branches ; and the giant , who could n't see what was going on behind him , had to carry the whole tree , and the little tailor into the bargain .
10 There he sat behind in the best of spirits , lustily whistling a tune , as if carrying the tree were mere sport .
11 The giant , after dragging the heavy weight for some time , could get on no further , and shouted out : `` Hi !
12 I must let the tree fall . ''
13 The tailor sprang nimbly down , seized the tree with both hands as if he had carried it the whole way and said to the giant : `` Fancy a big lout like you not being able to carry a tree ! ''
14 They continued to go on their way together , and as they passed by a cherry tree the giant grasped the top of it , where the ripest fruit hung , gave the branches into the tailor 's hand , and bade him eat .
15 But the little tailor was far too weak to hold the tree down , and when the giant let go the tree swung back into the air , bearing the little tailor with it .
16 When he had fallen to the ground again without hurting himself , the giant said : `` What !
17 do you mean to tell me you have n't the strength to hold down a feeble twig ? ''
18 `` It was n't strength that was wanting , '' replied the tailor ; `` do you think that would have been anything for a man who has killed seven at a blow ?
19 I jumped over the tree because the huntsmen are shooting among the branches near us .
20 Do you do the like if you dare . ''

8 They continued to go on their way together , and as they passed by a cherry tree the giant grasped the top of it , where the ripest fruit hung , gave the branches into the tailor 's hand , and bade him eat .
9 But the little tailor was far too weak to hold the tree down , and when the giant let go the tree swung back into the air , bearing the little tailor with it .
10 When he had fallen to the ground again without hurting himself , the giant said : `` What !
11 do you mean to tell me you have n't the strength to hold down a feeble twig ? ''
12 `` It was n't strength that was wanting , '' replied the tailor ; `` do you think that would have been anything for a man who has killed seven at a blow ?
13 I jumped over the tree because the huntsmen are shooting among the branches near us .
14 Do you do the like if you dare . ''
15 The giant made an attempt , but could n't get over the tree , and stuck fast in the branches , so that here too the little tailor had the better of him .
16 `` Well , you 're a fine fellow , after all , '' said the giant ; `` come and spend the night with us in our cave . ''
17 The little tailor willingly consented to do this , and following his friend they went on till they reached a cave where several other giants were sitting round a fire , each holding a roast sheep in his hand , of which he was eating .
18 The little tailor looked about him , and thought : `` Yes , there 's certainly more room to turn round in here than in my workshop . ''
19 The giant showed him a bed and bade him lie down and have a good sleep .
20 But the bed was too big for the little tailor , so he did n't get into it , but crept away into the corner .
21 At midnight , when the giant thought the little tailor was fast asleep , he rose up , and taking his big iron walking-stick , he broke the XXXXX in two with a blow , and thought he had made an end of the little grasshopper .	bed		attempt|bed|blow|man|part|spirits|sport|time|weight|workshop

12 While he lay there the people came , and looking him all over read on his girdle : `` Seven at a blow . ''
13 `` Oh ! ''
14 they said , `` what can this great hero of a hundred fights want in our peaceful land ?
15 He must indeed be a mighty man of valor . ''
16 They went and told the King about him , and said what a weighty and useful man he 'd be in time of war , and that it would be well to secure him at any price .
17 This counsel pleased the King , and he sent one of his courtiers down to the little tailor , to offer him , when he awoke , a commission in their army .
18 The messenger remained standing by the sleeper , and waited till he stretched his limbs and opened his eyes , when he tendered his proposal .
19 `` That 's the very thing I came here for , '' he answered ; `` I am quite ready to enter the King 's service . ''
20 So he was received with all honor , and given a special house of his own to live in .
21 But the other officers resented the success of the little XXXXX , and wished him a thousand miles away .	tailor		army|attempt|bed|corner|courtiers|feeling|honor|nose|room|tailor

1 `` Well , you 're a fine fellow , after all , '' said the giant ; `` come and spend the night with us in our cave . ''
2 The little tailor willingly consented to do this , and following his friend they went on till they reached a cave where several other giants were sitting round a fire , each holding a roast sheep in his hand , of which he was eating .
3 The little tailor looked about him , and thought : `` Yes , there 's certainly more room to turn round in here than in my workshop . ''
4 The giant showed him a bed and bade him lie down and have a good sleep .
5 But the bed was too big for the little tailor , so he did n't get into it , but crept away into the corner .
6 At midnight , when the giant thought the little tailor was fast asleep , he rose up , and taking his big iron walking-stick , he broke the bed in two with a blow , and thought he had made an end of the little grasshopper .
7 At early dawn the giants went off to the wood , and quite forgot about the little tailor , till all of a sudden they met him trudging along in the most cheerful manner .
8 The giants were terrified at the apparition , and , fearful lest he should slay them , they all took to their heels as fast as they could .

6 The messenger remained standing by the sleeper , and waited till he stretched his limbs and opened his eyes , when he tendered his proposal .
7 `` That 's the very thing I came here for , '' he answered ; `` I am quite ready to enter the King 's service . ''
8 So he was received with all honor , and given a special house of his own to live in .
9 But the other officers resented the success of the little tailor , and wished him a thousand miles away .
10 `` What 's to come of it all ? ''
11 they asked each other ; `` if we quarrel with him , he 'll let out at us , and at every blow seven will fall .
12 There 'll soon be an end of us . ''
13 So they resolved to go in a body to the King , and all to send in their papers .
14 `` We are not made , '' they said , `` to hold out against a man who kills seven at a blow . ''
15 The King was grieved at the thought of losing all his faithful servants for the sake of one man , and he wished heartily that he had never set eyes on him , or that he could get rid of him .
16 But he did n't dare to send him away , for he feared he might kill him along with his people , and place himself on the throne .
17 He pondered long and deeply over the matter , and finally came to a conclusion .
18 He sent to the tailor and told him that , seeing what a great and warlike hero he was , he was about to make him an offer .
19 In a certain wood of his kingdom there dwelled two giants who did much harm ; by the way they robbed , murdered , burned , and plundered everything about them ; `` no one could approach them without endangering his life .
20 But if he could overcome and kill these two giants he should have his only daughter for a wife , and half his kingdom into the bargain ; he might have a hundred horsemen , too , to back him up . ''
21 `` That 's the very thing for a man like me , '' thought the little tailor ; `` one does n't get the XXXXX of a beautiful princess and half a kingdom every day . ''	offer		courtiers|daughter|fights|miles|offer|people|tailor|thing|throne|wife

7 So they resolved to go in a body to the King , and all to send in their papers .
8 `` We are not made , '' they said , `` to hold out against a man who kills seven at a blow . ''
9 The King was grieved at the thought of losing all his faithful servants for the sake of one man , and he wished heartily that he had never set eyes on him , or that he could get rid of him .
10 But he did n't dare to send him away , for he feared he might kill him along with his people , and place himself on the throne .
11 He pondered long and deeply over the matter , and finally came to a conclusion .
12 He sent to the tailor and told him that , seeing what a great and warlike hero he was , he was about to make him an offer .
13 In a certain wood of his kingdom there dwelled two giants who did much harm ; by the way they robbed , murdered , burned , and plundered everything about them ; `` no one could approach them without endangering his life .
14 But if he could overcome and kill these two giants he should have his only daughter for a wife , and half his kingdom into the bargain ; he might have a hundred horsemen , too , to back him up . ''
15 `` That 's the very thing for a man like me , '' thought the little tailor ; `` one does n't get the offer of a beautiful princess and half a kingdom every day . ''
16 `` Done with you , '' he answered ; `` I 'll soon put an end to the giants .
17 But I have n't the smallest need of your hundred horsemen ; a fellow who can slay seven men at a blow need not be afraid of two . ''
18 The little tailor set out , and the hundred horsemen followed him .
19 When he came to the outskirts of the wood he said to his followers : `` You wait here , I 'll manage the giants by myself '' ; and he went on into the wood , casting his sharp little eyes right and left about him .
20 After a while he spied the two giants lying asleep under a tree , and snoring till the very boughs bent with the breeze .
21 The little XXXXX lost no time in filling his wallet with stones , and then climbed up the tree under which they lay .	tailor		body|breeze|everything|followers|horsemen|life|man|outskirts|papers|tailor

15 They both lay down to sleep again , and the tailor threw down a stone on the second giant , who sprang up and cried : `` What 's that for ?
16 Why did you throw something at me ? ''
17 `` I did n't throw anything , '' growled the first one .
18 They wrangled on for a time , till , as both were tired , they made up the matter and fell asleep again .
19 The little tailor began his game once more , and flung the largest stone he could find in his wallet with all his force , and hit the first giant on the chest .

1 He pondered long and deeply over the matter , and finally came to a conclusion .
2 He sent to the tailor and told him that , seeing what a great and warlike hero he was , he was about to make him an offer .
3 In a certain wood of his kingdom there dwelled two giants who did much harm ; by the way they robbed , murdered , burned , and plundered everything about them ; `` no one could approach them without endangering his life .
4 But if he could overcome and kill these two giants he should have his only daughter for a wife , and half his kingdom into the bargain ; he might have a hundred horsemen , too , to back him up . ''
5 `` That 's the very thing for a man like me , '' thought the little tailor ; `` one does n't get the offer of a beautiful princess and half a kingdom every day . ''
6 `` Done with you , '' he answered ; `` I 'll soon put an end to the giants .
7 But I have n't the smallest need of your hundred horsemen ; a fellow who can slay seven men at a blow need not be afraid of two . ''
8 The little tailor set out , and the hundred horsemen followed him .
9 When he came to the outskirts of the wood he said to his followers : `` You wait here , I 'll manage the giants by myself '' ; and he went on into the wood , casting his sharp little eyes right and left about him .
10 After a while he spied the two giants lying asleep under a tree , and snoring till the very boughs bent with the breeze .
11 The little tailor lost no time in filling his wallet with stones , and then climbed up the tree under which they lay .
12 When he got to about the middle of it he slipped along a branch till he sat just above the sleepers , when he threw down one stone after the other on the nearest giant .
13 The giant felt nothing for a long time , but at last he woke up , and pinching his companion said : `` What did you strike me for ? ''
14 `` I did n't strike you , '' said the other , `` you must be dreaming . ''

1 The little tailor set out , and the hundred horsemen followed him .
2 When he came to the outskirts of the wood he said to his followers : `` You wait here , I 'll manage the giants by myself '' ; and he went on into the wood , casting his sharp little eyes right and left about him .
3 After a while he spied the two giants lying asleep under a tree , and snoring till the very boughs bent with the breeze .
4 The little tailor lost no time in filling his wallet with stones , and then climbed up the tree under which they lay .
5 When he got to about the middle of it he slipped along a branch till he sat just above the sleepers , when he threw down one stone after the other on the nearest giant .
6 The giant felt nothing for a long time , but at last he woke up , and pinching his companion said : `` What did you strike me for ? ''
7 `` I did n't strike you , '' said the other , `` you must be dreaming . ''
8 They both lay down to sleep again , and the tailor threw down a stone on the second giant , who sprang up and cried : `` What 's that for ?
9 Why did you throw something at me ? ''
10 `` I did n't throw anything , '' growled the first one .
11 They wrangled on for a time , till , as both were tired , they made up the matter and fell asleep again .
12 The little tailor began his game once more , and flung the largest stone he could find in his wallet with all his force , and hit the first giant on the chest .
13 `` This is too much of a good thing ! ''
14 he yelled , and springing up like a madman , he knocked his companion against the tree till he trembled .
15 He gave , however , as good as he got , and they became so enraged that they tore up trees and beat each other with them , till they both fell dead at once on the ground .
16 Then the little tailor jumped down .
17 `` It 's a mercy , '' he said , `` that they did n't root up the tree on which I was perched , or I should have had to jump like a squirrel on to another , which , nimble though I am , would have been no easy job . ''
18 He drew his sword and gave each of the giants a very fine thrust or two on the breast , and then went to the horsemen and said : `` The deed is done , I 've put an end to the two of them ; but I assure you it has been no easy matter , for they even tore up trees in their struggle to defend themselves ; but all that 's of no use against one who slays seven men at a blow . ''
19 `` Were n't you wounded ? ''
20 asked the horsemen .
21 `` No fear , '' answered the XXXXX ; `` they have n't touched a hair of my head . ''	tailor		anything|branch|followers|giant|horsemen|mercy|middle|stone|tailor|wallet

15 The little tailor now demanded the promised reward from the King , but he repented his promise , and pondered once more how he could rid himself of the hero .
16 `` Before you obtain the hand of my daughter and half my kingdom , '' he said to him , `` you must do another deed of valor .
17 A unicorn is running about loose in the wood , and doing much mischief ; you must first catch it . ''
18 `` I 'm even less afraid of one unicorn than of two giants ; seven at a blow , that 's my motto . ''
19 He took a piece of cord and an axe with him , went out to the wood , and again told the men who had been sent with him to remain outside .
20 He had n't to search long , for the unicorn soon passed by , and , on perceiving the tailor , dashed straight at him as though it were going to spike him on the spot .
21 `` Gently , gently , '' said he , `` not so fast , my friend '' ; and standing still he waited till the beast was quite near , when he sprang lightly behind a tree ; the unicorn ran with all its XXXXX against the tree , and rammed its horn so firmly into the trunk that it had no strength left to pull it out again , and was thus successfully captured .	force		anything|chest|force|hair|hand|something|struggle|sword|tree|wallet

1 Why did you throw something at me ? ''
2 `` I did n't throw anything , '' growled the first one .
3 They wrangled on for a time , till , as both were tired , they made up the matter and fell asleep again .
4 The little tailor began his game once more , and flung the largest stone he could find in his wallet with all his force , and hit the first giant on the chest .
5 `` This is too much of a good thing ! ''
6 he yelled , and springing up like a madman , he knocked his companion against the tree till he trembled .
7 He gave , however , as good as he got , and they became so enraged that they tore up trees and beat each other with them , till they both fell dead at once on the ground .
8 Then the little tailor jumped down .
9 `` It 's a mercy , '' he said , `` that they did n't root up the tree on which I was perched , or I should have had to jump like a squirrel on to another , which , nimble though I am , would have been no easy job . ''
10 He drew his sword and gave each of the giants a very fine thrust or two on the breast , and then went to the horsemen and said : `` The deed is done , I 've put an end to the two of them ; but I assure you it has been no easy matter , for they even tore up trees in their struggle to defend themselves ; but all that 's of no use against one who slays seven men at a blow . ''
11 `` Were n't you wounded ? ''
12 asked the horsemen .
13 `` No fear , '' answered the tailor ; `` they have n't touched a hair of my head . ''
14 But the horsemen would n't believe him till they rode into the wood and found the giants weltering in their blood , and the trees lying around , torn up by the roots .

15 `` Gently , gently , '' said he , `` not so fast , my friend '' ; and standing still he waited till the beast was quite near , when he sprang lightly behind a tree ; the unicorn ran with all its force against the tree , and rammed its horn so firmly into the trunk that it had no strength left to pull it out again , and was thus successfully captured .
16 `` Now I 've caught my bird , '' said the tailor , and he came out from behind the tree , placed the cord round its neck first , then struck the horn out of the tree with his axe , and when everything was in order led the beast before the King .
17 Still the King did n't want to give him the promised reward and made a third demand .
18 The tailor was to catch a wild boar for him that did a great deal of harm in the wood ; and he might have the huntsmen to help him .
19 `` Willingly , '' said the tailor ; `` that 's mere child 's play . ''
20 But he did n't take the huntsmen into the wood with him , and they were well enough pleased to remain behind , for the wild boar had often received them in a manner which did not make them desire its further acquaintance .
21 As soon as the boar perceived the XXXXX it ran at him with foaming mouth and gleaming teeth , and tried to knock him down ; but our alert little friend ran into a chapel that stood near , and got out of the window again with a jump .	tailor		acquaintance|breast|fear|horsemen|men|play|reward|roots|tailor|trunk

1 He gave , however , as good as he got , and they became so enraged that they tore up trees and beat each other with them , till they both fell dead at once on the ground .
2 Then the little tailor jumped down .
3 `` It 's a mercy , '' he said , `` that they did n't root up the tree on which I was perched , or I should have had to jump like a squirrel on to another , which , nimble though I am , would have been no easy job . ''
4 He drew his sword and gave each of the giants a very fine thrust or two on the breast , and then went to the horsemen and said : `` The deed is done , I 've put an end to the two of them ; but I assure you it has been no easy matter , for they even tore up trees in their struggle to defend themselves ; but all that 's of no use against one who slays seven men at a blow . ''
5 `` Were n't you wounded ? ''
6 asked the horsemen .
7 `` No fear , '' answered the tailor ; `` they have n't touched a hair of my head . ''
8 But the horsemen would n't believe him till they rode into the wood and found the giants weltering in their blood , and the trees lying around , torn up by the roots .
9 The little tailor now demanded the promised reward from the King , but he repented his promise , and pondered once more how he could rid himself of the hero .
10 `` Before you obtain the hand of my daughter and half my kingdom , '' he said to him , `` you must do another deed of valor .
11 A unicorn is running about loose in the wood , and doing much mischief ; you must first catch it . ''
12 `` I 'm even less afraid of one unicorn than of two giants ; seven at a blow , that 's my motto . ''
13 He took a piece of cord and an axe with him , went out to the wood , and again told the men who had been sent with him to remain outside .
14 He had n't to search long , for the unicorn soon passed by , and , on perceiving the tailor , dashed straight at him as though it were going to spike him on the spot .

17 Still the King did n't want to give him the promised reward and made a third demand .

12 `` Now I 've caught my bird , '' said the tailor , and he came out from behind the tree , placed the cord round its neck first , then struck the horn out of the tree with his axe , and when everything was in order led the beast before the King .
13 Still the King did n't want to give him the promised reward and made a third demand .
14 The tailor was to catch a wild boar for him that did a great deal of harm in the wood ; and he might have the huntsmen to help him .
15 `` Willingly , '' said the tailor ; `` that 's mere child 's play . ''
16 But he did n't take the huntsmen into the wood with him , and they were well enough pleased to remain behind , for the wild boar had often received them in a manner which did not make them desire its further acquaintance .
17 As soon as the boar perceived the tailor it ran at him with foaming mouth and gleaming teeth , and tried to knock him down ; but our alert little friend ran into a chapel that stood near , and got out of the window again with a jump .
18 The boar pursued him into the church , but the tailor skipped round to the door , and closed it securely .
19 So the raging beast was caught , for it was far too heavy and unwieldy to spring out of the window .
20 The little tailor summoned the huntsmen together , that they might see the prisoner with their own eyes .
21 Then the hero betook himself to the King , who was obliged now , whether he liked it or not , to keep his promise , and hand him over his daughter and half his XXXXX .	kingdom		acquaintance|blood|boar|demand|head|kingdom|motto|prisoner|promise|tree

1 He had n't to search long , for the unicorn soon passed by , and , on perceiving the tailor , dashed straight at him as though it were going to spike him on the spot .
2 `` Gently , gently , '' said he , `` not so fast , my friend '' ; and standing still he waited till the beast was quite near , when he sprang lightly behind a tree ; the unicorn ran with all its force against the tree , and rammed its horn so firmly into the trunk that it had no strength left to pull it out again , and was thus successfully captured .
3 `` Now I 've caught my bird , '' said the tailor , and he came out from behind the tree , placed the cord round its neck first , then struck the horn out of the tree with his axe , and when everything was in order led the beast before the King .
4 Still the King did n't want to give him the promised reward and made a third demand .
5 The tailor was to catch a wild boar for him that did a great deal of harm in the wood ; and he might have the huntsmen to help him .
6 `` Willingly , '' said the tailor ; `` that 's mere child 's play . ''
7 But he did n't take the huntsmen into the wood with him , and they were well enough pleased to remain behind , for the wild boar had often received them in a manner which did not make them desire its further acquaintance .
8 As soon as the boar perceived the tailor it ran at him with foaming mouth and gleaming teeth , and tried to knock him down ; but our alert little friend ran into a chapel that stood near , and got out of the window again with a jump .
9 The boar pursued him into the church , but the tailor skipped round to the door , and closed it securely .
10 So the raging beast was caught , for it was far too heavy and unwieldy to spring out of the window .
11 The little tailor summoned the huntsmen together , that they might see the prisoner with their own eyes .
12 Then the hero betook himself to the King , who was obliged now , whether he liked it or not , to keep his promise , and hand him over his daughter and half his kingdom .
13 Had he known that no hero-warrior , but only a little tailor stood before him , it would have gone even more to his heart .
14 So the wedding was celebrated with much splendor and little joy , and the tailor became a king .
15 After a time the Queen heard her husband saying one night in his sleep : `` My lad , make that waistcoat and patch these trousers , or I 'll box your ears . ''
16 Thus she learned in what rank the young gentleman had been born , and next day she poured forth her woes to her father , and begged him to help her to get rid of a husband who was nothing more nor less than a tailor .
17 The King comforted her , and said : `` Leave your bedroom door open to-night , my servants shall stand outside , and when your husband is fast asleep they shall enter , bind him fast , and carry him on to a ship , which shall sail away out into the wide ocean . ''
18 The Queen was well satisfied with the idea , but the armor-bearer , who had overheard everything , being much attached to his young master , went straight to him and revealed the whole plot .
19 `` I 'll soon put a stop to the business , '' said the tailor .
20 That night he and his wife went to bed at the usual time ; and when she thought he had fallen asleep she got up , opened the door , and then lay down again .
21 The little tailor , who had only pretended to be asleep , began to call out in a clear voice : `` My lad , make that waistcoat and patch those XXXXX , or I 'll box your ears .	trousers		acquaintance|everything|father|hand|horn|master|mouth|trousers|trunk|waistcoat

14 Thus she learned in what rank the young gentleman had been born , and next day she poured forth her woes to her father , and begged him to help her to get rid of a husband who was nothing more nor less than a tailor .

17 `` I 'll soon put a stop to the business , '' said the tailor .
18 That night he and his wife went to bed at the usual time ; and when she thought he had fallen asleep she got up , opened the door , and then lay down again .

19 A VOYAGE TO LILLIPUT CHAPTER I My father had a small estate in Nottinghamshire , and I was the third of four sons .
20 He sent me to Cambridge at fourteen years old , and after studying there three years I was bound apprentice to Mr. Bates , a famous surgeon in London .
21 There , as my XXXXX now and then sent me small sums of money , I spent them in learning navigation , and other arts useful to those who travel , as I always believed it would be some time or other my fortune to do .	father		business|everything|father|king|mouth|nothing|plot|tailor|waistcoat|wedding

15 I have killed seven at a blow , slain two giants , led a unicorn captive , and caught a wild boar , then why should I be afraid of those men standing outside my door ? ''
16 The men , when they heard the tailor saying these words , were so terrified that they fled as if pursued by a wild army , and did n't dare go near him again .
17 So the little tailor was and remained a king all the days of his life .
18 A VOYAGE TO LILLIPUT CHAPTER I My father had a small estate in Nottinghamshire , and I was the third of four sons .
19 He sent me to Cambridge at fourteen years old , and after studying there three years I was bound apprentice to Mr. Bates , a famous surgeon in London .
20 There , as my father now and then sent me small sums of money , I spent them in learning navigation , and other arts useful to those who travel , as I always believed it would be some time or other my fortune to do .
21 Three years after my leaving him my good XXXXX , Mr. Bates , recommended me as ship 's surgeon to the `` Swallow , '' on which I voyaged three years .	master		army|beast|chapel|friend|hero|joy|jump|master|promise|window

1 So the raging beast was caught , for it was far too heavy and unwieldy to spring out of the window .
2 The little tailor summoned the huntsmen together , that they might see the prisoner with their own eyes .
3 Then the hero betook himself to the King , who was obliged now , whether he liked it or not , to keep his promise , and hand him over his daughter and half his kingdom .
4 Had he known that no hero-warrior , but only a little tailor stood before him , it would have gone even more to his heart .
5 So the wedding was celebrated with much splendor and little joy , and the tailor became a king .
6 After a time the Queen heard her husband saying one night in his sleep : `` My lad , make that waistcoat and patch these trousers , or I 'll box your ears . ''
7 Thus she learned in what rank the young gentleman had been born , and next day she poured forth her woes to her father , and begged him to help her to get rid of a husband who was nothing more nor less than a tailor .
8 The King comforted her , and said : `` Leave your bedroom door open to-night , my servants shall stand outside , and when your husband is fast asleep they shall enter , bind him fast , and carry him on to a ship , which shall sail away out into the wide ocean . ''
9 The Queen was well satisfied with the idea , but the armor-bearer , who had overheard everything , being much attached to his young master , went straight to him and revealed the whole plot .
10 `` I 'll soon put a stop to the business , '' said the tailor .
11 That night he and his wife went to bed at the usual time ; and when she thought he had fallen asleep she got up , opened the door , and then lay down again .
12 The little tailor , who had only pretended to be asleep , began to call out in a clear voice : `` My lad , make that waistcoat and patch those trousers , or I 'll box your ears .
13 I have killed seven at a blow , slain two giants , led a unicorn captive , and caught a wild boar , then why should I be afraid of those men standing outside my door ? ''
14 The men , when they heard the tailor saying these words , were so terrified that they fled as if pursued by a wild army , and did n't dare go near him again .
15 So the little tailor was and remained a king all the days of his life .
16 A VOYAGE TO LILLIPUT CHAPTER I My father had a small estate in Nottinghamshire , and I was the third of four sons .
17 He sent me to Cambridge at fourteen years old , and after studying there three years I was bound apprentice to Mr. Bates , a famous surgeon in London .
18 There , as my father now and then sent me small sums of money , I spent them in learning navigation , and other arts useful to those who travel , as I always believed it would be some time or other my fortune to do .
19 Three years after my leaving him my good master , Mr. Bates , recommended me as ship 's surgeon to the `` Swallow , '' on which I voyaged three years .
20 When I came back I settled in London , and , having taken part of a small house , I married Miss Mary Burton , daughter of Mr. Edmund Burton , hosier .
21 But my good XXXXX Bates died two years after ; and as I had few friends my business began to fail , and I determined to go again to sea .	master		box|captive|eyes|hand|king|kingdom|master|promise|rank|voice

7 A VOYAGE TO LILLIPUT CHAPTER I My father had a small estate in Nottinghamshire , and I was the third of four sons .
8 He sent me to Cambridge at fourteen years old , and after studying there three years I was bound apprentice to Mr. Bates , a famous surgeon in London .
9 There , as my father now and then sent me small sums of money , I spent them in learning navigation , and other arts useful to those who travel , as I always believed it would be some time or other my fortune to do .
10 Three years after my leaving him my good master , Mr. Bates , recommended me as ship 's surgeon to the `` Swallow , '' on which I voyaged three years .
11 When I came back I settled in London , and , having taken part of a small house , I married Miss Mary Burton , daughter of Mr. Edmund Burton , hosier .
12 But my good master Bates died two years after ; and as I had few friends my business began to fail , and I determined to go again to sea .
13 After several voyages , I accepted an offer from Captain W. Pritchard , master of the `` Antelope , '' who was making a voyage to the South Sea .
14 We set sail from Bristol , May 4 , 1699 ; and our voyage at first was very prosperous .
15 But in our passage to the East Indies we were driven by a violent storm to the north-west of Van Diemen 's Land .
16 Twelve of our crew died from hard labor and bad food , and the rest were in a very weak condition .
17 On the 5th of November , the weather being very hazy , the seamen spied a rock within 120 yards of the ship ; but the wind was so strong that we were driven straight upon it , and immediately split .
18 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
19 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
20 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .

14 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
15 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
16 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
17 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
18 By this time the storm was much abated .
19 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
20 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
21 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my XXXXX for about nine hours .	life		Antelope|crew|father|heat|king|life|mercy|offer|time|weather

1 When I came back I settled in London , and , having taken part of a small house , I married Miss Mary Burton , daughter of Mr. Edmund Burton , hosier .
2 But my good master Bates died two years after ; and as I had few friends my business began to fail , and I determined to go again to sea .
3 After several voyages , I accepted an offer from Captain W. Pritchard , master of the `` Antelope , '' who was making a voyage to the South Sea .
4 We set sail from Bristol , May 4 , 1699 ; and our voyage at first was very prosperous .
5 But in our passage to the East Indies we were driven by a violent storm to the north-west of Van Diemen 's Land .
6 Twelve of our crew died from hard labor and bad food , and the rest were in a very weak condition .
7 On the 5th of November , the weather being very hazy , the seamen spied a rock within 120 yards of the ship ; but the wind was so strong that we were driven straight upon it , and immediately split .
8 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
9 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
10 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
11 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
12 By this time the storm was much abated .
13 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
14 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
15 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
16 When I woke , it was just daylight .
17 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
18 I could only look upward .
19 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
20 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
21 In a little XXXXX I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .	time		May|business|condition|depth|ground|offer|rock|squall|tide|time

9 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .

14 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
15 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
16 When I woke , it was just daylight .
17 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
18 I could only look upward .
19 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
20 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
21 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my XXXXX downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .	eyes		business|condition|eyes|grass|hair|legs|master|noise|nothing|squall

1 But in our passage to the East Indies we were driven by a violent storm to the north-west of Van Diemen 's Land .
2 Twelve of our crew died from hard labor and bad food , and the rest were in a very weak condition .
3 On the 5th of November , the weather being very hazy , the seamen spied a rock within 120 yards of the ship ; but the wind was so strong that we were driven straight upon it , and immediately split .
4 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
5 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
6 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
7 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
8 By this time the storm was much abated .
9 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
10 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
11 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
12 When I woke , it was just daylight .
13 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
14 I could only look upward .
15 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
16 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
17 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
18 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
19 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
20 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
21 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the XXXXX ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .	ground		back|fortune|ground|hair|leg|life|mercy|mile|squall|vessel

1 But in our passage to the East Indies we were driven by a violent storm to the north-west of Van Diemen 's Land .

1 Twelve of our crew died from hard labor and bad food , and the rest were in a very weak condition .
2 On the 5th of November , the weather being very hazy , the seamen spied a rock within 120 yards of the ship ; but the wind was so strong that we were driven straight upon it , and immediately split .
3 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
4 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
5 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
6 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
7 By this time the storm was much abated .
8 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
9 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
10 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
11 When I woke , it was just daylight .
12 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
13 I could only look upward .
14 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
15 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
16 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
17 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
18 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
19 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
20 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .

1 On the 5th of November , the weather being very hazy , the seamen spied a rock within 120 yards of the ship ; but the wind was so strong that we were driven straight upon it , and immediately split .
2 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
3 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
4 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
5 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
6 By this time the storm was much abated .
7 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
8 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
9 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
10 When I woke , it was just daylight .
11 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
12 I could only look upward .
13 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
14 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
15 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
16 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
17 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
18 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
19 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .
20 But the creatures ran off a second time before I could seize them , whereupon there was a great shout , and in an instant I felt above a hundred arrows discharged on my left hand , which pricked me like so many needles .

1 On the 5th of November , the weather being very hazy , the seamen spied a rock within 120 yards of the ship ; but the wind was so strong that we were driven straight upon it , and immediately split .
2 Six of the crew , of whom I was one , letting down the boat , got clear of the ship , and we rowed about three leagues , till we could work no longer .
3 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
4 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
5 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
6 By this time the storm was much abated .
7 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
8 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
9 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .

12 I could only look upward .
13 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
14 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
15 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
16 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
17 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .

15 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
16 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
17 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .
18 But the creatures ran off a second time before I could seize them , whereupon there was a great shout , and in an instant I felt above a hundred arrows discharged on my left hand , which pricked me like so many needles .
19 Moreover , they shot another flight into the air , of which some fell on my face , which I immediately covered with my left hand .
20 When this shower of arrows was over I groaned with grief and pain , and then , striving again to get loose , they discharged another flight of arrows larger than the first , and some of them tried to stab me with their spears ; but by good luck I had on a leather jacket , which they could not pierce .
21 By this XXXXX I thought it most prudent to lie still till night , when , my left hand being already loose , I could easily free myself ; and as for the inhabitants , I thought I might be a match for the greatest army they could bring against me if they were all of the same size as him I saw .	time		creature|depth|hand|inches|nothing|rock|time|uneasiness|weather|wind

1 We therefore trusted ourselves to the mercy of the waves ; and in about half an hour the boat was upset by a sudden squall .
2 What became of my companions in the boat , or those who escaped on the rock or were left in the vessel , I can not tell ; but I conclude they were all lost .
3 For my part , I swam as fortune directed me , and was pushed forward by wind and tide ; but when I was able to struggle no longer I found myself within my depth .
4 By this time the storm was much abated .
5 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .
6 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
7 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
8 When I woke , it was just daylight .
9 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
10 I could only look upward .
11 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
12 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
13 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
14 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
15 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
16 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
17 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .
18 But the creatures ran off a second time before I could seize them , whereupon there was a great shout , and in an instant I felt above a hundred arrows discharged on my left hand , which pricked me like so many needles .
19 Moreover , they shot another flight into the air , of which some fell on my face , which I immediately covered with my left hand .
20 When this shower of arrows was over I groaned with grief and pain , and then , striving again to get loose , they discharged another flight of arrows larger than the first , and some of them tried to stab me with their spears ; but by good luck I had on a leather jacket , which they could not pierce .
21 By this time I thought it most prudent to lie still till night , when , my left XXXXX being already loose , I could easily free myself ; and as for the inhabitants , I thought I might be a match for the greatest army they could bring against me if they were all of the same size as him I saw .	hand		bow|hand|head|hurt|length|life|nothing|shore|sides|something

3 By this time the storm was much abated .
4 I reached the shore at last , about eight o'clock in the evening , and advanced nearly half a mile inland , but could not discover any sign of inhabitants .

7 When I woke , it was just daylight .
8 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
9 I could only look upward .
10 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
11 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
12 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
13 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
14 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
15 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
16 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .
17 But the creatures ran off a second time before I could seize them , whereupon there was a great shout , and in an instant I felt above a hundred arrows discharged on my left hand , which pricked me like so many needles .
18 Moreover , they shot another flight into the air , of which some fell on my face , which I immediately covered with my left hand .
19 When this shower of arrows was over I groaned with grief and pain , and then , striving again to get loose , they discharged another flight of arrows larger than the first , and some of them tried to stab me with their spears ; but by good luck I had on a leather jacket , which they could not pierce .
20 By this time I thought it most prudent to lie still till night , when , my left hand being already loose , I could easily free myself ; and as for the inhabitants , I thought I might be a match for the greatest army they could bring against me if they were all of the same size as him I saw .
21 When the people observed that I was quiet they discharged no more XXXXX , but by the noise I heard I knew that their number was increased ; and about four yards from me , for more than an hour , there was a knocking , like people at work .	arrows		army|arrows|creature|grass|hands|needles|pain|sides|something|uneasiness

19 Then , turning my head that way as well as the pegs and strings would let me , I saw a stage set up , about a foot and a half from the ground , with two or three ladders to mount it .
20 From this , one of them , who seemed to be a person of quality , made me a long speech , of which I could not understand a word , though I could tell from his manner that he sometimes threatened me , and sometimes spoke with pity and kindness .

9 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
10 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
11 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .

17 When the people observed that I was quiet they discharged no more arrows , but by the noise I heard I knew that their number was increased ; and about four yards from me , for more than an hour , there was a knocking , like people at work .
18 Then , turning my head that way as well as the pegs and strings would let me , I saw a stage set up , about a foot and a half from the ground , with two or three ladders to mount it .
19 From this , one of them , who seemed to be a person of quality , made me a long speech , of which I could not understand a word , though I could tell from his manner that he sometimes threatened me , and sometimes spoke with pity and kindness .
20 I answered in few words , but in the most submissive manner ; and , being almost famished with hunger , I could not help showing my impatience by putting my finger frequently to my mouth , to signify that I wanted food .
21 He understood me very well , and , descending from the XXXXX , commanded that several ladders should be set against my sides , on which more than a hundred of the inhabitants mounted , and walked toward my mouth with baskets full of food , which had been sent by the King 's orders when he first received tidings of me .	stage		arm|bow|face|hair|legs|sight|something|stage|strings|weather

18 Then , turning my head that way as well as the pegs and strings would let me , I saw a stage set up , about a foot and a half from the ground , with two or three ladders to mount it .
19 From this , one of them , who seemed to be a person of quality , made me a long speech , of which I could not understand a word , though I could tell from his manner that he sometimes threatened me , and sometimes spoke with pity and kindness .
20 I answered in few words , but in the most submissive manner ; and , being almost famished with hunger , I could not help showing my impatience by putting my finger frequently to my mouth , to signify that I wanted food .
21 He understood me very well , and , descending from the stage , commanded that several ladders should be set against my XXXXX , on which more than a hundred of the inhabitants mounted , and walked toward my mouth with baskets full of food , which had been sent by the King 's orders when he first received tidings of me .	sides		air|breast|hunger|inhabitants|manner|mouth|nothing|side|sides|strings

1 I was extremely tired , and with the heat of the weather I found myself much inclined to sleep .
2 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
3 When I woke , it was just daylight .
4 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
5 I could only look upward .
6 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
7 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
8 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
9 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
10 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
11 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
12 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .
13 But the creatures ran off a second time before I could seize them , whereupon there was a great shout , and in an instant I felt above a hundred arrows discharged on my left hand , which pricked me like so many needles .
14 Moreover , they shot another flight into the air , of which some fell on my face , which I immediately covered with my left hand .
15 When this shower of arrows was over I groaned with grief and pain , and then , striving again to get loose , they discharged another flight of arrows larger than the first , and some of them tried to stab me with their spears ; but by good luck I had on a leather jacket , which they could not pierce .
16 By this time I thought it most prudent to lie still till night , when , my left hand being already loose , I could easily free myself ; and as for the inhabitants , I thought I might be a match for the greatest army they could bring against me if they were all of the same size as him I saw .
17 When the people observed that I was quiet they discharged no more arrows , but by the noise I heard I knew that their number was increased ; and about four yards from me , for more than an hour , there was a knocking , like people at work .
18 Then , turning my head that way as well as the pegs and strings would let me , I saw a stage set up , about a foot and a half from the ground , with two or three ladders to mount it .
19 From this , one of them , who seemed to be a person of quality , made me a long speech , of which I could not understand a word , though I could tell from his manner that he sometimes threatened me , and sometimes spoke with pity and kindness .
20 I answered in few words , but in the most submissive manner ; and , being almost famished with hunger , I could not help showing my impatience by putting my finger frequently to my mouth , to signify that I wanted food .
21 He understood me very well , and , descending from the stage , commanded that several ladders should be set against my sides , on which more than a hundred of the XXXXX mounted , and walked toward my mouth with baskets full of food , which had been sent by the King 's orders when he first received tidings of me .	inhabitants		bow|breast|face|half|head|inhabitants|mouth|people|pull|yards

1 I lay down on the grass , which was very short and soft , and slept sounder than ever I did in my life for about nine hours .
2 When I woke , it was just daylight .
3 I attempted to rise , but could not ; for as I happened to be lying on my back , I found my arms and legs were fastened on each side to the ground ; and my hair , which was long and thick , tied down in the same manner .
4 I could only look upward .
5 The sun began to grow hot , and the light hurt my eyes .
6 I heard a confused noise about me , but could see nothing except the sky .
7 In a little time I felt something alive and moving on my left leg , which , advancing gently over my breast , came almost up to my chin , when , bending my eyes downward , I perceived it to be a human creature , not six inches high , with a bow and arrow in his hands , and a quiver at his back .
8 In the meantime I felt at least forty more following the first .
9 I was in the utmost astonishment , and roared so loud that they all ran back in a fright ; and some of them were hurt with the falls they got by leaping from my sides upon the ground .
10 However , they soon returned , and one of them , who ventured so far as to get a full sight of my face , lifted up his hands in admiration .
11 I lay all this while in great uneasiness ; but at length , struggling to get loose , I succeeded in breaking the strings that fastened my left arm to the ground ; and at the same time , with a violent pull that gave me extreme pain , I a little loosened the strings that tied down my hair , so that I was just able to turn my head about two inches .
12 But the creatures ran off a second time before I could seize them , whereupon there was a great shout , and in an instant I felt above a hundred arrows discharged on my left hand , which pricked me like so many needles .
13 Moreover , they shot another flight into the air , of which some fell on my face , which I immediately covered with my left hand .
14 When this shower of arrows was over I groaned with grief and pain , and then , striving again to get loose , they discharged another flight of arrows larger than the first , and some of them tried to stab me with their spears ; but by good luck I had on a leather jacket , which they could not pierce .
15 By this time I thought it most prudent to lie still till night , when , my left hand being already loose , I could easily free myself ; and as for the inhabitants , I thought I might be a match for the greatest army they could bring against me if they were all of the same size as him I saw .
16 When the people observed that I was quiet they discharged no more arrows , but by the noise I heard I knew that their number was increased ; and about four yards from me , for more than an hour , there was a knocking , like people at work .
17 Then , turning my head that way as well as the pegs and strings would let me , I saw a stage set up , about a foot and a half from the ground , with two or three ladders to mount it .
18 From this , one of them , who seemed to be a person of quality , made me a long speech , of which I could not understand a word , though I could tell from his manner that he sometimes threatened me , and sometimes spoke with pity and kindness .
19 I answered in few words , but in the most submissive manner ; and , being almost famished with hunger , I could not help showing my impatience by putting my finger frequently to my mouth , to signify that I wanted food .
20 He understood me very well , and , descending from the stage , commanded that several ladders should be set against my sides , on which more than a hundred of the inhabitants mounted , and walked toward my mouth with baskets full of food , which had been sent by the King 's orders when he first received tidings of me .
21 There were XXXXX and shoulders like mutton but smaller than the wings of a lark .	legs		chin|hunger|hurt|inches|inhabitants|leg|legs|shout|side|something
